16 function hasConnection() {

17 return navigator.connection || navigator.mozConnection || navigator.webkitConnection || navigator.msConnection;

18 }

19

20 function createLink(src) {

21 var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");

22 preloadLink.rel = "preload";

23 preloadLink.href = src;

24 preloadLink.as = "font";

25 preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";

26 preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";

27 document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);

28 }

29

30 var connectionDetail = {

31 saveDataEnabled: false,

32 slowConnection: false

33 };

34

35 var connection = hasConnection();

36 if (connection) {

37 connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled = connection.saveData;

38 if (/\slow-2g|2g/.test(connection.effectiveType)) {

39 connectionDetail.slowConnection = true;

40 }

41 }

42

304 }

306 this.ready = function () {};

307 }

308 },

309

310 insert: function (s) {

311 var t = d.getElementById('js-position' + this.index);

312 if (t && t.insertAdjacentElement) {

313 t.insertAdjacentElement('afterend', s);

314 } else {

315 d.head.appendChild(s);

316 }

317 ++this.index;

318 },

319

320 createScript: function (script, beforeLoad) {

321 var s = d.createElement('script');

322 s.id = 'js-position' + (this.index + 1);

323 s.setAttribute('data-test', script.test);

324 if (beforeLoad) {

325 s.defer = 'defer';

326 s.onload = function () {

327 if (script.noinit) {

328 loader.register(true);

329 }

330 if (d.readyState === 'interactive' || d.readyState === 'complete') {

331 loader.ready();

332 }

333 };

334 } else {

335 s.async = 'async';

336 }

337 s.src = script.src;

338 return s;

339 },

340

341 init: function () {

342 this.scripts.forEach(function (s) {

343 loader.insert(loader.createScript(s, true));

344 });

345

346 d.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {

347 loader.ready();

348

349 var conditionalScripts = [

350 {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es6-bundle-464a2af269.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js', module: true },

351 {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es5-bundle-2626f1bdf8.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js', nomodule: true },

352 {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es6-bundle-d616f8e4f9.js', test: 'math-js', module: true},

353 {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es5-bundle-66109c5184.js', test: 'math-js', nomodule: true}

354 ];

355

356

357 if (conditionalScripts) {

358 conditionalScripts.filter(function (script) {

359 return !!document.querySelector(script.match) && !((moduleSupport && script.nomodule) || (!moduleSupport && script.module));

360 }).forEach(function (script) {

361 loader.insert(loader.createScript(script));

362 });

363 }

364 }, false);

365 }

366 };

367 loader.init();

368 }

369 })(window, document);

370</script>

1257 <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="">

1258Models, mechanisms and clinical evidence for cancer dormancy</h1>

1259

1260 <ul class="c-article-author-list c-article-author-list--short" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list">

1261<li class="c-article-author-list__item">

1262<a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Julio_A_-Aguirre_Ghiso-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Julio_A_-Aguirre_Ghiso-Aff1">

1263Julio A. Aguirre-Ghiso</a>

1367

1368 <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Key Points" lang="en">

1369<div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section">

1370<h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">

1371Key Points</h2>

1372<div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content">

1373

1374 <ul class="u-list-style-bullet">

1375

1376 <li>

1377

1378 <p>

1379In the clinic, tumour dormancy is observed in local recurrences or metastases. It usually refers to the time after treatment that a patient is asymptomatic but still carries local remnant or disseminated tumour cells that do not grow into overt lesions.</p>

1380

1381 </li>

1382

1383 <li>

1384

1385 <p>

1386Tumour dormancy ensues when cancer cell proliferation is counteracted by other mechanisms such as apoptosis because of impaired vascularization or immunosurveillance, and cellular dormancy ensues when the cancer cells enter a growth arrest.</p>

1387

1388 </li>

1389

1390 <li>

1391

1392 <p>

1393Cancer dormancy is a relevant problem because the majority of solid tumours and haematological malignancies undergo a period of dormancy that is characterized by years to decades of minimal residual disease. Because metastases always arise from disseminated tumour cells it is of importance to understand the biology of dormant tumour cells.</p>

1394

1395 </li>

1396

1397 <li>

1398

1399 <p>

1400Several mechanisms can explain cancer dormancy. These include the disruption of crosstalk between growth factor and adhesion signalling, which prevents tumour cells from interpreting their microenvironment, leading to cellular tumour dormancy through a G0G1 arrest or 'differentiation'; the inability of a tumour cell population to recruit blood vessels despite active proliferation; and immunosurveillance, which can prevent residual tumour cell expansion.</p>

1401

1402 </li>

1403

1404 <li>

1405

1406 <p>

1407The expression of genes that selectively suppress metastases might function by inducing dormancy. In addition, quiescent tumour 'stem' cells might be dormant tumour cells. Finally, dormant tumour cells seem to have active drug resistance mechanisms that might protect them from therapy.</p>

1408

1409 </li>

1410

1411 <li>

1412

1413 <p>

1414The therapeutic opportunities that emerge from understanding dormancy include the possibility of inducing and/or maintaining the dormancy of tumour cells and inducing cell death in residual dormant cells by targeting their survival and drug-resistance mechanisms.</p>

1415

1416 </li>

1417

1418 <li>

1419

1420 <p>

1421Studies of cancer dormancy might help determine whether a patient has dormant disease and what type of mechanism is active. These studies will be instrumental in identifying biomarkers of dormant cancer.</p>

1422

1423 </li>

1424

1425 </ul>

1426

1427 </div>

1428</div>

1429</section>

1430<section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en">

1431<div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section">

1432<h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">

1433Abstract</h2>

1434<div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content">

1435<p>

1436Patients with cancer can develop recurrent metastatic disease with latency periods that range from years even to decades. This pause can be explained by cancer dormancy, a stage in cancer progression in which residual disease is present but remains asymptomatic. Cancer dormancy is poorly understood, resulting in major shortcomings in our understanding of the full complexity of the disease. Here, I review experimental and clinical evidence that supports the existence of various mechanisms of cancer dormancy including angiogenic dormancy, cellular dormancy (G0G1 arrest) and immunosurveillance. The advances in this field provide an emerging picture of how cancer dormancy can ensue and how it could be therapeutically targeted.</p>

1437</div>

1438</div>

1439</section>

5397<section data-title="Glossary">

5398<div class="c-article-section" id="glossary-section">

5399<h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="glossary">

5400Glossary</h2>

5401<div class="c-article-section__content" id="glossary-content">

5402<dl class="c-description-list">

5403<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos1">

5404Disseminated tumour cells</dt>

5405<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5406

5407 <p>

5408Tumour cells that have physically separated from the primary tumour and spread to other anatomical locations through circulation. In this Review these tumour cells are not yet considered micrometastases as they have not yet expanded to form a small population of cells.</p>

5409

5410 </dd>

5411<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos2">

5412Minimal residual disease</dt>

5413<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5414

5415 <p>

5416Remnant tumour cells that are left after treatment and that cannot be detected by conventional clinical testing. These cells can persist in the primary site or as disseminated tumour cells in proliferative and/or dormant phases.</p>

5417

5418 </dd>

5419<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos3">

5420Dormant</dt>

5421<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5422

5423 <p>

5424Applies to cells in a non-dividing state whose physiological functions become paused or quiescent. In several organisms, such as <i>

5425Caenorhabditis elegans</i>

5426, dormant is a synonym for diapause (pause in development). Plant seeds are also dormant during the latency phase before germination.</p>

5427

5428 </dd>

5429<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos4">

5430Tumour mass dormancy</dt>

5431<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5432

5433 <p>

5434Cancer cell proliferation that is counterbalanced by apoptosis owing to poor vascularization (see angiogenic dormancy) or by an immune response. In this case the cancer cells are never truly inactive, but rather are incapable of expanding beyond a certain number.</p>

5435

5436 </dd>

5437<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos5">

5438Cellular dormancy</dt>

5439<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5440

5441 <p>

5442When normal cells enter the G0 phase of the cell cycle and have low metabolism. This might apply to cancer cells that enter a G0G1 arrest. This form of dormancy is clinically asymptomatic, and the tumour cells are truly inactive.</p>

5443

5444 </dd>

5445<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos6">

5446Angiogenic dormancy</dt>

5447<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5448

5449 <p>

5450Occurs when cancer cell proliferation is counterbalanced by apoptosis owing to poor vascularization. In this case, the cancer cells are incapable of expanding beyond a certain number and although clinically asymptomatic the tumour cells are not truly inactive.</p>

5451

5452 </dd>

5453<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos7">

5454Genetically fit</dt>

5455<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5456

5457 <p>

5458The concept of evolutionary genetic fitness. Having the appropriate traits and properties to survive and grow in a particular environment.</p>

5459

5460 </dd>

5461<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos8">

5462Immortalization</dt>

5463<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5464

5465 <p>

5466Cells with genetic alterations that become insensitive to the Hayflick limit (the number of times a normal cell can divide <i>

5467in vitro</i>

5468 before dying or entering senescence). Immortalized cells evade senescence and proliferate limitlessly in culture but are unable to form tumours.</p>

5469

5470 </dd>

5471<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos9">

5472Transformation</dt>

5473<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5474

5475 <p>

5476Cells that have been immortalized but now acquire additional genetic and epigenetic alterations that allow them to form primary tumours.</p>

5477

5478 </dd>

5479<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos10">

5480Micrometastasis</dt>

5481<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5482

5483 <p>

5484A small group of tumour cells, derived from a disseminated tumour cell, that has grown in secondary organs but is too small to be seen or detected by available methods. These lesions might also be clinically asymptomatic.</p>

5485

5486 </dd>

5487<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos11">

5488Orthotopic</dt>

5489<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5490

5491 <p>

5492The growth of transplanted cells or tissues in the normal anatomical position and tissue of origin.</p>

5493

5494 </dd>

5495<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos12">

5496Niche</dt>

5497<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5498

5499 <p>

5500A term borrowed from ecology, it refers to a unique and optimal tissue microenvironment with defined nurturing and positional cues in a given anatomical location that allows a cell or group of cells to survive and function.</p>

5501

5502 </dd>

5503<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos13">

5504Reconstituted basement membrane assays</dt>

5505<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5506

5507 <p>

5508Assays that take advantage of extracellular matrix gels that are derived from a cancer cell line, or collagen-I gels. Cells embedded in these matrices can recapitulate the tissue organization that is observed in the tissue of origin.</p>

5509

5510 </dd>

5511<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos14">

5512Acinar structures</dt>

5513<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5514

5515 <p>

5516Sac-like structures that are formed by secretory epithelial cells that have defined apico-basal polarity.</p>

5517

5518 </dd>

5519<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos15">

5520Fibronectin</dt>

5521<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5522

5523 <p>

5524An extracellular matrix molecule that is a main constituent of normal tissues but is also present in serum. The 51 integrin is its most specific receptor (other integrins can also bind it) and it can serve as a survival or mitogenic factor.</p>

5525

5526 </dd>

5527<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos16">

5528CD8<sup>

5529+</sup>

5530 T lymphocytes</dt>

5531<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5532

5533 <p>

5534Immune cells from the T-cell lineage specialized in killing target cells (that is, virus-infected cells). These responses are usually mediated by class-I major histocompatibility complexes and cytotoxicity is delivered by a repertoire of molecules that are contained in secretory vesicles.</p>

5535

5536 </dd>

5537<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos17">

5538Neovascularization</dt>

5539<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5540

5541 <p>

5542The formation of new blood vessels that create new pathways for blood flow during normal tissue development or remodelling, or during pathological conditions such as tumour growth or retinopathy.</p>

5543

5544 </dd>

5545<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos18">

5546Angiogenic switch</dt>

5547<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5548

5549 <p>

5550When a small tumour mass senses the lack of appropriate blood supply and activates a series of transcriptional programmes that allows them to produce signals that will recruit new blood vessels.</p>

5551

5552 </dd>

5553<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos19">

5554Adaptation</dt>

5555<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5556

5557 <p>

5558Mechanisms that allow normal or tumour cells to respond to a determined stress with a programme that allows them to correct and survive the imposed stress signals.</p>

5559

5560 </dd>

5561<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos20">

5562Dauer stage</dt>

5563<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5564

5565 <p>

5566German for 'enduring'. It is an alternative larval stage in which development is arrested in response to environmental or hormonal cues in nematodes such as <i>

5567Caenorhabditis elegans</i>

5568. It allows larvae to survive harsh conditions for long periods until the environment is propitious to resume development.</p>

5569

5570 </dd>

5571<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos21">

5572Unfolded protein response</dt>

5573<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5574

5575 <p>

5576A cellular response to stress that is unique to the endoplasmic reticulum (ER), and which senses the misfolding of proteins in the ER. It activates a series of pathways that help the cells survive proteotoxicity that is caused by unfolded proteins or activate mechanisms of cell death.</p>

5577

5578 </dd>

5579<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos22">

5580Humoral response</dt>

5581<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5582

5583 <p>

5584The component of the immure response that is mediated by secreted antibodies.</p>

5585

5586 </dd>

5587<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos23">

5588Anti-idiotypic antibodies</dt>

5589<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5590

5591 <p>

5592Antibodies that are directed towards the hypervariable regions of an antibody.</p>

5593

5594 </dd>

5595<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos24">

5596Oestrogen starvation</dt>

5597<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5598

5599 <p>

5600Occurs when cells that are usually dependent on oestrogen for normal cellular functions are deprived of this hormone. This is usually done in cell culture by leaving oestrogen out of the tissue culture medium.</p>

5601

5602 </dd>

5603<dt class="c-description-list__term u-text-bold" id="Glos25">

5604ABC transporters</dt>

5605<dd class="c-description-list__description u-pl-32">

5606

5607 <p>

5608ATP-dependent protein pumps that extrude numerous compounds from the cell cytoplasm. These can be exogenous molecules such as drugs, or physiological membrane constituents such as cholesterol.</p>

5609

5610 </dd>

5611</dl>

5612</div>

5613</div>

5614</section>
